      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty">
        Poverty
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">
        World Bank
       </a>
       categorizes Honduras as a low middle-income nation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The nation’s per capita income sits at around 600 US dollars making it one of the lowest in North America.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2010, 50% of the population were still living below the
       <a href="/wiki/Poverty_threshold" title="Poverty threshold">
        poverty line
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 2016 more than 66% was living below the poverty line.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Estimates put
       <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">
        unemployment
       </a>
       at about 27.9%, which is more than 1.2 million Hondurans.
      </p>
      <p>
       Economic growth in the last few years has averaged 7% a year, one of the highest rates in Latin America (2010).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_64-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite this, Honduras has seen the least development amongst all Central American countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduras is ranked 130 of 188 countries with a
       <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">
        Human Development Index
       </a>
       of .625 that classifies the nation as having medium development (2015).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The three factors that go into Honduras' HDI (an extended and healthy life, accessibility of knowledge and standard of living) have all improved since 1990 but still remain relatively low with life expectancy at birth being 73.3, expected years of schooling being 11.2 (mean of 6.2 years) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GNP_per_capita" title="GNP per capita">
        GNI per capita
       </a>
       being $4,466 (2015).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The HDI for Latin America and the Caribbean overall is 0.751 with life expectancy at birth being 68.6, expected years of schooling being 11.5 (mean of 6.6) and GNI per capita being $6,281 (2015).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/2009_Honduran_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2009 Honduran coup d'état">
        2009 Honduran coup d'état
       </a>
       led to a variety of economic trends in the nation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Overall growth has slowed, averaging 5.7 percent from 2006-2008 but slowing to 3.5 percent annually between 2010 and 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Following the coup trends of decreasing poverty and extreme poverty were reversed. The nation saw a poverty increase of 13.2 percent and in extreme poverty of 26.3 percent in just 3 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, unemployment grew between 2008 and 2012 from 6.8 percent to 14.1 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because much of the Honduran economy is based on small scale agriculture of only a few exports, natural disasters have a particularly devastating impact. Natural disasters such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Mitch" title="Hurricane Mitch">
        1998 Hurricane Mitch
       </a>
       have contributed to this inequality as they particularly affecting poor rural areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, they are a large contributor to food insecurity in the country as farmers are left unable to provide for their families.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A study done by Honduras NGO, World Neighbors, determined the tems "increased workload, decreased basic grains, expensive food, and fear" were most associated with Hurricane Mitch.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The rural and urban poor were hit hardest by Hurricane Mitch.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Those in Southern and Western regions specifically were considered most vulnerable as they both subject environmental destruction and home to many sustenance farmers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to disasters such as Hurricane Mitch, the agricultural economic sector has declined a third in the past twenty years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is mostly due to a decline in exports such as banana and coffee that were affected by factors such as natural disasters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Indigenous communities along the Patuca River were hit extremely hard as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mid-Pataca region was almost completely destroyed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over 80% of rice harvest and all of banana, plantain, and manioc harvests were lost.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Relief and reconstruction efforts following the storm were partial and incomplete reinforcing existing levels of poverty rather than reversing it, especially for indigenous communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The period between the end food donations and the following harvest led to extreme hunger causing deaths amongst the Tawahka population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Those that were considered the most “land-rich” lost 36% of their total land on average.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Those that were the most “land-poor,” lost less total land but a greater share of their overall total.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This meant that those hit hardest were single women as their constitute the majority of this population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_8-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty_reduction_strategies">
        Poverty reduction strategies
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Since the 1970s when Honduras was designated a “food priority country” by the UN, organizations such as The World Food Program (WFP) have worked to decrease malnutrition and food insecurity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www1.wfp.org_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-www1.wfp.org-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A large majority of Honduran famers live in
       <a href="/wiki/Extreme_poverty" title="Extreme poverty">
        extreme poverty
       </a>
       , or below 180 US dollars per capita.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Currently one fourth of children are affected by chronic malnutrition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www1.wfp.org_71-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-www1.wfp.org-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       WFP is currently working with the Honduran government on a School Feeding Program which provides meals for 21,000 Honduran schools, reaching 1.4 million school children.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www1.wfp.org_71-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-www1.wfp.org-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       WFP also participates in disaster relief through reparations and emergency response in order to aid in quick recovery that tackles the effects of natural disasters on agricultural production.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www1.wfp.org_71-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-www1.wfp.org-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Honduras’
       <a href="/wiki/Poverty_Reduction_Strategy_in_Honduras" title="Poverty Reduction Strategy in Honduras">
        Poverty Reduction Strategy
       </a>
       was implemented in 1999 and aimed to cut
       <a href="/wiki/Extreme_poverty" title="Extreme poverty">
        extreme poverty
       </a>
       in half by 2015.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econstor.eu_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-econstor.eu-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While spending on poverty-reduction aid  increased there was only a 2.5% increase in GDP between 1999 and 2002.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:10-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This improvement left Honduras still below that of countries that lacked aid through Poverty Reduction Strategy behind those without it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_74-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:10-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The World Bank believes that this inefficiency stems from a lack of focus on infrastructure and rural development.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_74-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:10-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Extreme_poverty" title="Extreme poverty">
        Extreme poverty
       </a>
       saw a low of 36.2 percent only two years after the implementation of the strategy but then increased to 66.5 percent by 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Poverty Reduction Strategies were also intended to affect social policy through increased investment in education and health sectors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:11-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was expected to lift poor communities out of poverty while also increasing the work force as a means of stimulating the Honduran economy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_75-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:11-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Conditional cash transfers were used to do this by the Family Assistance Program.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_75-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:11-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This program was restructured in 1998 in an attempt to increase effectiveness of cash transfers for health and education specifically for those in extreme poverty.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_75-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:11-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Overall spending within Poverty Reduction Strategies have been focused on education and health sectors increasing social spending from 44% of Honduras' GDP in 2000 to 51% in 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_75-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:11-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Critics of aid from International Finance Institutions believe that the World Bank's Poverty Reduction Strategy result in little substantive change to Honduran policy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:11_75-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:11-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Poverty Reduction Strategies also excluded clear priorities, specific intervention strategy, strong commitment to the strategy and more effective macro-level economic reforms according to Jose Cuesta of Cambridge University.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_74-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:10-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to this he believes that the strategy did not provide a pathway for economic development that could lift Honduras out of poverty resulting in neither lasting economic growth of poverty reduction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_74-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:10-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Prior to its 2009
       <a href="/wiki/2009_Honduran_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2009 Honduran coup d'état">
        coup
       </a>
       Honduras widely expanded social spending and an extreme increase in minimum wage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Efforts to decrease inequality were swiftly reversed following the coup.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When Zelaya was removed from office social spending as a percent of GDP decreased from 13.3 percent in 2009 to 10.9 recent in 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This decrease in social spending exacerbated the effects of the recession, which the nation was previously relatively well equipped to deal with.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/World_Bank_Group" title="World Bank Group">
        World Bank
       </a>
       Group Executive Board approved a plan known as the new Country Partnership Framework (CPF).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This plan’s objectives are to expand social program coverage, strengthen infrastructure, increase financing accessibility, strengthen regulatory framework and institutional capacity, improve the productivity of rural areas, strengthen natural disaster and climate change resiliency, and the buildup local governments so that violence and crime rates will decrease.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_64-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The overall aim of the initiative is to decrease inequality and vulnerability of certain populations while increasing economic growth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank.org_69-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldbank.org-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally the signing of the U.S.-Central America Free Trade Agreement (CAFTA) was meant to diversify the economy in order to promote growth and expand the range of exports the country is reliant on.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econstor.eu_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-econstor.eu-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_inequality">
        Economic inequality
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Levels of income inequality in Honduras are higher than in any other Latin American country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike other Latin American countries, inequality steadily increased in Honduras between 1991 and 2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econstor.eu_73-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-econstor.eu-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Between 2006 and 2010 inequality saw a decrease but increased again in 2010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elcorreo.eu.org_68-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-elcorreo.eu.org-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Honduras'
       <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">
        Human Development Index
       </a>
       is adjusted for inequality (known as the IHDI) Honduras' development index is reduced to .443.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The levels of inequality in each aspect of development can also be assessed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2015 inequality of life expectancy at birth was 19.6%, inequality in education was 24.4% and inequality in income was 41.5%
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The overall loss in human development due to inequality was 29.2.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The IHDI for Latin America and the Caribbean overall is 0.575 with an overall loss of 23.4%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2015 for the entire region, inequality of life expectancy at birth was 22.9%, inequality in education was 14.0% and inequality in income was 34.9%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While Honduras has a higher life expectancy than other countries in the region (before and after inequality adjustments), its quality of education and economic standard of living are lower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Income inequality and education inequality have a large impact on the overall development of the nation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Inequality also exists between rural and urban areas as it relates to the distribution of resources.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Poverty is concentrated in southern, eastern, and western regions where rural and indigenous peoples live. North and central Honduras are home to the country's industries and infrastructure, resulting in low levels of poverty.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_65-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:2-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Poverty is concentrated in rural Honduras, a pattern that is reflected throughout Latin America.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The effects of poverty on rural communities are vast. Poor communities typically live in adobe homes, lack material resources, have limited access to medical resources, and live off of basics such as rice, maize and beans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The lower class predominantly consists of rural subsistence farmers and landless peasants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since 1965 there has been an increase in the number of landless peasants in Honduras which has led to a growing class of urban poor individuals.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These individuals often migrate to urban centers in search of work in the service sector, manufacturing, or construction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Demographers believe that without social and economic reform, rural to urban migration will increase, resulting in the expansion of urban centers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Within the lower class, underemployment is a major issue.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Individuals that are underemployed often only work as part-time laborers on seasonal farms meaning their annual income remains low.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1980s peasant organizations and labor unions such as the National Federation of Honduran Peasants, The National Association of Honduran Peasants and the National Union of Peasants formed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is not uncommon for rural individuals to voluntarily enlist in the military, however this often does not offer stable or promising career opportunities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:8-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The majority of high-ranking officials in the Honduran army are recruited from elite military academies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_79-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:8-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, the majority of enlistment in the military is forced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_79-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:8-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Forced recruitment largely relies on an alliance between the Honduran government, military and upper class Honduran society.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_79-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:8-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In urban areas males are often sought out from secondary schools while in rural areas roadblocks aided the military in handpicking recruits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_79-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:8-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Higher socio-economic status enables individuals to more easily evade the draft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_79-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:8-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Middle class Honduras is a small group defines by relatively low membership and income levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Movement from lower to middle class is typically facilitated by higher education.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Professionals, students, farmers, merchants, business employees, and civil servants are all considered a part of the Honduran middle class.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Opportunities for employment and the industrial and commercial sectors are slow growing, limiting middle class membership.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Honduran upper class has much higher income levels than the rest of the Honduran population reflecting large amounts of income inequality.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Much of the upper class affords their success to the growth of cotton and livestock exports post-
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The wealthy are not politically unified and differ in political and economic views.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">
        Trade
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The currency is the
       <a href="/wiki/Honduran_lempira" title="Honduran lempira">
        Honduran lempira
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The government operates both the electrical grid,
       <a href="/wiki/Empresa_Nacional_de_Energ%C3%ADa_El%C3%A9ctrica" title="Empresa Nacional de Energía Eléctrica">
        Empresa Nacional de Energía Eléctrica
       </a>
       (ENEE) and the land-line telephone service,
       <a href="/wiki/Hondutel" title="Hondutel">
        Hondutel
       </a>
       . ENEE receives heavy
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsidies" title="Subsidies">
        subsidies
       </a>
       to counter its chronic financial problems, but Hondutel is no longer a
       <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">
        monopoly
       </a>
       . The telecommunication sector was opened to private investment on 25 December 2005, as required under
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAFTA" title="CAFTA">
        CAFTA
       </a>
       . The price of petroleum is regulated, and the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Congress_of_Honduras" title="National Congress of Honduras">
        Congress
       </a>
       often ratifies temporary
       <a href="/wiki/Price" title="Price">
        price
       </a>
       regulation for basic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commodities" title="Commodities">
        commodities
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Gold, silver, lead and zinc are mined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cafta_countries.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="344" data-file-width="458" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Cafta_countries.png/220px-Cafta_countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Cafta_countries.png/330px-Cafta_countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Cafta_countries.png/440px-Cafta_countries.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cafta_countries.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         CAFTA countries
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2005 Honduras signed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAFTA" title="CAFTA">
        CAFTA
       </a>
       , a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_trade_agreement" title="Free trade agreement">
        free trade agreement
       </a>
       with the United States. In December 2005,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Cortes" title="Puerto Cortes">
        Puerto Cortes
       </a>
       , the primary seaport of Honduras, was included in the U.S.
       <a href="/wiki/Container_Security_Initiative" title="Container Security Initiative">
        Container Security Initiative
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2006 the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Homeland_Security" title="U.S. Department of Homeland Security">
        U.S. Department of Homeland Security
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
        Department of Energy
       </a>
       announced the first phase of the
       <a href="/wiki/Secure_Freight_Initiative" title="Secure Freight Initiative">
        Secure Freight Initiative
       </a>
       (SFI), which built upon existing port security measures. SFI gave the U.S. government enhanced authority, allowing it to scan containers from overseas
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2016)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       for nuclear and radiological materials in order to improve the risk assessment of individual US-bound containers. The initial phase of Secure Freight involved deploying of nuclear detection and other devices to six foreign ports:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Port_Qasim" title="Port Qasim">
         Port Qasim
        </a>
        in Pakistan;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerto_Cortes" title="Puerto Cortes">
         Puerto Cortes
        </a>
        in Honduras;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">
         Southampton
        </a>
        in the United Kingdom;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Salalah" title="Port of Salalah">
         Port of Salalah
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">
         Oman
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Singapore" title="Port of Singapore">
         Port of Singapore
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        Gamman Terminal at
        <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Busan" title="Port of Busan">
         Port Busan
        </a>
        , Korea.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Containers in these ports have been scanned since 2007 for radiation and other risk factors before they are allowed to depart for the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       For economic development a 2012 memorandum of understanding with a group of international investors obtained Honduran government approval to build a zone (city) with its own laws, tax system, judiciary and police, but opponents brought a suit against it in the Supreme Court, calling it a "state within a state".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2013, Honduras' Congress ratified Decree 120, which led to the establishment of
       <a href="/wiki/Zone_for_Employment_and_Economic_Development_(Honduras)" title="Zone for Employment and Economic Development (Honduras)">
        ZEDEs
       </a>
       . The government began construction of the first zones in June 2015.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">
        Energy
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_Honduras" title="Electricity sector in Honduras">
        Electricity sector in Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       About half of the electricity sector in Honduras is privately owned. The remaining
       <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">
        generation
       </a>
       capacity is run by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ENEE" title="ENEE">
        ENEE
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Empresa Nacional de Energía Eléctrica
       </i>
       ). Key challenges in the sector are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Financing investments in generation and transmission without either a financially healthy utility or concessionary funds from external donors
       </li>
       <li>
        Re-balancing
        <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">
         tariffs
        </a>
        , cutting
        <a href="/wiki/Arrears" title="Arrears">
         arrears
        </a>
        and reducing losses, including electricity theft, without social unrest
       </li>
       <li>
        Reconciling environmental concerns with government objectives – two large new dams and associated hydropower plants.
       </li>
       <li>
        Improving access to electricity in rural areas.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">
        Transport
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carretera37.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="1024" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Carretera37.jpg/300px-Carretera37.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Carretera37.jpg/450px-Carretera37.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Carretera37.jpg/600px-Carretera37.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carretera37.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A highway in Honduras
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Infrastructure for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Honduras" title="Transportation in Honduras">
        transportation in Honduras
       </a>
       consists of: 699 kilometres (434 miles)
       <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Honduras" title="Rail transport in Honduras">
        of railways
       </a>
       ; 13,603 kilometres (8,453 miles) of roadways;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-cia-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       seven ports and harbors;
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2007)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       and 112 airports altogether (12 Paved, 100 unpaved).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-cia-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Ministry of Public Works, Transport and Housing (SOPRTRAVI in Spanish acronym) is responsible for transport sector policy.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">
        Water supply and sanitation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Honduras" title="Water supply and sanitation in Honduras">
        Water supply and sanitation in Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Honduras" title="Water supply and sanitation in Honduras">
        Water supply and sanitation in Honduras
       </a>
       differ greatly from urban centers to rural villages. Larger population centers generally have modernized water treatment and distribution systems, but water quality is often poor because of lack of proper maintenance and treatment. Rural areas generally have basic drinking water systems with limited capacity for water treatment. Many urban areas have sewer systems in place to collect wastewater, but proper treatment of wastewater is rare. In rural areas sanitary facilities are generally limited to latrines and basic septic pits.
      </p>
      <p>
       Water and sanitation services were historically provided by the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Servicio_Aut%C3%B3nomo_de_Alcantarillas_y_Aqueductos&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Servicio Autónomo de Alcantarillas y Aqueductos (page does not exist)">
        Servicio Autónomo de Alcantarillas y Aqueductos
       </a>
       <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
        (
        <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Servicio_Aut%C3%B3nomo_de_Alcantarillas_y_Aqueductos" title="es:Servicio Autónomo de Alcantarillas y Aqueductos">
         es
        </a>
        )
       </span>
       (SANAA). In 2003, the government enacted a new "water law" which called for the
       <a href="/wiki/Decentralization" title="Decentralization">
        decentralization
       </a>
       of water services. Under the 2003 law, local communities have both the right and the responsibility to own, operate, and control their own drinking water and wastewater systems. Since this law passed, many communities have joined together to address water and sanitation issues on a regional basis.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many national and international non-government organizations have a history of working on water and sanitation projects in Honduras. International groups include the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement" title="International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement">
        Red Cross
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Water_1st_International" title="Water 1st International">
        Water 1st
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rotary_International" title="Rotary International">
        Rotary Club
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Relief_Services" title="Catholic Relief Services">
        Catholic Relief Services
       </a>
       , Water for People,
       <a href="/wiki/EcoLogic_Development_Fund" title="EcoLogic Development Fund">
        EcoLogic Development Fund
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CARE_(relief)" title="CARE (relief)">
        CARE
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Executive_Service_Organization" title="Canadian Executive Service Organization">
        Canadian Executive Service Organization
       </a>
       (CESO-SACO),
       <a href="/wiki/Engineers_Without_Borders_%E2%80%93_USA" title="Engineers Without Borders – USA">
        Engineers Without Borders – USA
       </a>
       , Flood The Nations,
       <a href="/wiki/Students_Helping_Honduras" title="Students Helping Honduras">
        Students Helping Honduras
       </a>
       (SHH),
       <a href="/wiki/Global_Brigades" title="Global Brigades">
        Global Brigades
       </a>
       , and Agua para el Pueblo
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in partnership with
       <a href="/wiki/AguaClara" title="AguaClara">
        AguaClara
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">
        Cornell University
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition, many government organizations work on projects in Honduras, including the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">
        USAID
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">
        Army Corps of Engineers
       </a>
       , Cooperacion Andalucia, the
       <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">
        government of Japan
       </a>
       , and others.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Honduras" title="Demographics of Honduras">
        Demographics of Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Honduras had a population of 9,112,867 in 2016.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The proportion of the population below the age of 15 in 2010 was 36.8%, 58.9% were between 15 and 65 years old, and 4.3% were 65 years old or older.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPP_2012_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WPP_2012-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since 1975, emigration from Honduras has accelerated as economic migrants and political refugees sought a better life elsewhere. A majority of
       <a href="/wiki/Honduran_diaspora" title="Honduran diaspora">
        expatriate Hondurans
       </a>
       live in the United States. A 2012 US State Department estimate suggested that between 800,000 and one million Hondurans lived in the United States at that time, nearly 15% of the Honduran population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOS-Honduras_39-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-DOS-Honduras-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The large uncertainty about numbers is because numerous Hondurans live in the United States without a visa. In the 2010 census in the United States, 617,392 residents identified as Hondurans, up from 217,569 in 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Race_and_Ethnicity">
        Race and Ethnicity
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The ethnic breakdown of Honduran society was 90%
       <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">
        Mestizo
       </a>
       , 7% American Indian, 2% Black and 1% White (2017).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1927 Honduran census provides no racial data but in 1930 five classifications were created: white, Indian, Negro, yellow, and mestizo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This system was used in the 1935 and 1940 census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_88-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Mestizo was used to describe individuals that did not fit neatly into the categories of white, Indian, negro or yellow or who are of mixed white-Indian descent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_88-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       John Gillin considers Honduras to be one of thirteen "Mestizo countries" (Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Nicaragua, Panama, Colombia, Venezuela, Cuba, Ecuador, Peru, Bolivia, Paraguay).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:5-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He claims that in much as Spanish America little attention is paid to race and race mixture resulting in social status having little reliance on one's physical features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:5-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in "Mestizo countries" such as Honduras, this is not the case.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_89-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:5-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Social stratification from Spain was able to develop in these countries through colonization.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_89-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:5-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During colonization the majority of Honduras' indigenous population was killed or died of disease resulting in a more homogenous indigenous population compared to other colonies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nine indigenous and African American groups are recognized by the government in Honduras.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:6-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The majority of Amerindians in Honduras are
       <a href="/wiki/Lenca_people" title="Lenca people">
        Lenca
       </a>
       , followed by the Miskito, Cho’rti’, Tolupan, Pech and Sumo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_90-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:6-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Around 50,000 Lenca individuals live in the west and western interior of Honduras while the other small native groups are located throughout the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The majority of blacks in Honduran are culturally
       <a href="/wiki/Ladino_people" title="Ladino people">
        ladino
       </a>
       , meaning they are culturally Hispanic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Non-ladino groups in Honduras include the
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Carib" title="Black Carib">
        Black Carib
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Miskito_people" title="Miskito people">
        Miskito
       </a>
       , Arab immigrants and the black population of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islas_de_la_Bah%C3%ADa" title="Islas de la Bahía">
        Islas de la Bahía
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Black Carib population descended from freed slaves from Saint Vincent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Miskito population (about 10,000 individuals) are the descendants of African and British immigrants and are extremely racially diverse.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While the Black Carib and Miskito populations have similar origins, Black Caribs are considered black while Miskitos are considered indigenous.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is largely a reflection of cultural differences, as Black Caribs have retained much of their original African culture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The majority of Arab Hondurans are of Palestinian and Lebanese descent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are known as "turcos" in Honduras because of migration during the rule of the Ottoman Empire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They have maintained cultural distinctiveness and prospered economically.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_78-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">
        Gender
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality_in_Honduras" title="Gender inequality in Honduras">
        Gender inequality in Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The male to female ratio of the Honduran population is 1.01. This ratio stands at 1.05 at birth, 1.04 from 15–24 years old, 1.02 from 25–54 years old, .88 from 55–64 years old, and .77 for those 65 years or older.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Gender_Development_Index" title="Gender Development Index">
        The Gender Development Index
       </a>
       (GDI) was .942 in 2015 with an HDI of .600 for females and .637 for males.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Life expectancy at birth for males is 70.9 and 75.9 for females.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Expected years of schooling in Honduras is 10.9 years for males (mean of 6.1) and 11.6 for females (mean of 6.2).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These measures do not reveal a large disparity between male and female development levels, however, GNI per capita is vastly different by gender.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Males have a GNI per capita of $6,254 while that of females is only $2,680.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduras' overall GDI is higher than that of other medium HDI nations (.871) but lower than the overall HDI for Latin America and the Caribbean (.981).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The United Nations Development Program (UNDP) ranks Honduras 116th for measures including women’s political power, and female access to resources.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:15-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Gender_Inequality_Index" title="Gender Inequality Index">
        The Gender Inequality Index
       </a>
       (GII) depicts gender-based inequalities in Honduras according to reproductive health, empowerment, and economic activity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduras has a GII of .461 and ranked 101 of 159 countries in 2015.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       25.8% of Honduras' parliament is female and 33.4% of adult females have a secondary education of higher while only 31.1% of adult males do.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite this, while male participation in the labor market is 84.4, female participation is 47.2%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduras'
       <a href="/wiki/Maternal_Mortality_Ratio" title="Maternal Mortality Ratio">
        maternal mortality ratio
       </a>
       is 129 and the adolescent birth rate is 65.0 for women ages 15–19.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_10-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-:1-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Familialism" title="Familialism">
        Familialism
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Machismo" title="Machismo">
        machismo
       </a>
       carry a lot of weight within Honduran society.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:14-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Familialism refers to the idea of individual interests being second to that of the family, most often in relation to dating and marriage, abstinence, and parental approval and supervision of dating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_91-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:14-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Aggression and proof of masculinity through physical dominance are characteristic of machismo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:14_91-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:14-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Honduras has historically functioned with a patriarchal system like many other Latin American countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduran men claim responsibility for family decisions including reproductive health decisions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recently Honduras has seen an increase in challenges to this notion as feminist movements and access to global media increases.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There has been an increase in educational attainment, labor force participating, urban migration, late-age marriage, and contraceptive use amongst Honduran women.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 1971 and 2001 Honduran total fertility rate decreased from 7.4 births to 4.4 births.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is largely attributable to an increase in educational attainment and workforce participation by women, as well as more widespread use of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contraceptives" title="Contraceptives">
        contraceptives
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1996 50% of women were using at least one type of contraceptive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 2001 62% were largely due to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_sterilization" title="Female sterilization">
        female sterilization
       </a>
       , birth control in the form of a pill, injectable birth control, and IUDs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A study done in 2001 of Honduran men and women reflect conceptualization of reproductive health and decision making in Honduras.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       28% of men and 25% of women surveyed believed men were responsible for decisions regarding family size and family planning uses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       21% of men believed men were responsible for both.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_92-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-:12-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Sexual violence against women has proven to be a large issue in Honduras that has caused many to migrate to the U.S.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The prevalence of child sexual abuse was 7.8% in Honduras with the majority of reports being from children under the age of 11.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:18-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Women that experienced sexual abuse as children were found to be twice as likely to be in violent relationships.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:18_94-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:18-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Femicide is widespread in Honduras.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2014, 40% of unaccompanied refugee minors were female.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Gangs are largely responsible for sexual violence against women as they often use sexual violence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Between 2005 and 2013 according to the UN Special Repporteur on Violence Against Women, violent deaths increased 263.4 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Impunity for sexual violence and femicide crimes was 95 percent in 2014.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, many girls are forced into human trafficking and prostitution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:16_93-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-:16-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Between 1995 and 1997 Honduras recognized domestic violence as both a public health issue and a punishable offense due to efforts by the Pan American Health Organization (PAHO).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:15-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       PAHO’s subcommittee on Women, Health and Development was used as a guide to develop programs that aid in domestic violence prevention and victim assistance programs
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:15_14-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:15-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, a study done in 2009 showed that while the policy requires health care providers to report cases of sexual violence, emergency contraception, and victim referral to legal institutions and support groups, very few other regulations exist within the realm of registry, examination and follow-up.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:17-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike other Central American countries such as El Salvador, Guatemala and Nicaragua, Honduras does not have detailed guidelines requiring service providers to be extensively trained and respect the rights of sexual violence victims.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_95-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:17-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the study was done the UNFPA  and the Health Secretariat of Honduras have worked to develop and implement improved guidelines for handling cases of sexual violence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:17_95-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:17-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An educational program in Honduras known as
       <i>
        Sistema de Aprendizaje Tutorial
       </i>
       (SAT) has attempted to "undo gender" through focusing on gender equality in everyday interactions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:13-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduras' SAT program is one of the largest in the world, second only to Colombia's with 6,000 students.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_96-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:13-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is currently sponsored by
       <i>
        Asociacion Bayan
       </i>
       , a Honduran NGO, and the Honduran Ministry of Education.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_96-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:13-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It functions by integrating gender into curriculum topics, linking gender to the ideas of justice and equality, encouraging reflection, dialogue and debate and emphasizing the need for individual and social change.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_96-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:13-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This program was found to increase gender consciousness and a desire for gender equality amongst Honduran women through encouraging discourse surrounding existing gender inequality in the Honduran communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:13_96-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:13-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">
        Languages
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In addition to Spanish a number of indigenous languages are spoken in Honduras, as well as Honduran sign language and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bay_Islands_Creole_English" title="Bay Islands Creole English">
        Bay Islands Creole English
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The main indigenous languages are:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Garifuna_language" title="Garifuna language">
         Garifuna
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Arawakan_languages" title="Arawakan languages">
         Arawakan
        </a>
        ) (almost 100,000 speakers in Honduras including monolinguals)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%ADskito_language" title="Mískito language">
         Mískito
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Misumalpan_languages" title="Misumalpan languages">
         Misumalpan
        </a>
        ) (29,000 speakers in Honduras)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumu_language" title="Sumu language">
         Mayangna
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Misumalpan_languages" title="Misumalpan languages">
         Misumalpan
        </a>
        ) (less than 1000 speakers in Honduras, more in
        <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">
         Nicaragua
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pech_language" title="Pech language">
         Pech/Paya
        </a>
        , (
        <a href="/wiki/Chibchan_languages" title="Chibchan languages">
         Chibchan
        </a>
        ) (less than 1000 speakers)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jicaque_language" title="Jicaque language">
         Tol
        </a>
        (isolate) (less than 500 speakers)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ch%E2%80%99orti%E2%80%99_language" title="Ch’orti’ language">
         Ch’orti’
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">
         Mayan
        </a>
        ) (less than 50 speakers)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lenca_language" title="Lenca language">
        Lenca
       </a>
       isolate lost all its fluent native speakers in the 20th century but is currently undergoing revival efforts among the members of the ethnic population of about 100,000. The largest immigrant languages are Arabic (42,000), Armenian (1,300), Turkish (900), Yue Chinese (1,000).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
        Religion
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Honduras" title="Religion in Honduras">
        Religion in Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="516" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg/170px-OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg/255px-OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg/340px-OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OscarKardinalRodriguez.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Cardinal
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%93scar_Andr%C3%A9s_Rodr%C3%ADguez" title="Óscar Andrés Rodríguez">
          Óscar Andrés Rodríguez
         </a>
         is
         <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Tegucigalpa" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Tegucigalpa">
          Archbishop of Tegucigalpa
         </a>
         and a figure of national and international note
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although most Hondurans are nominally
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">
        Roman Catholic
       </a>
       (which would be considered the main religion), membership in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
        Roman Catholic Church
       </a>
       is declining while membership in Protestant churches is increasing. The International Religious Freedom Report, 2008, notes that a CID Gallup poll reported that 51.4% of the population identified themselves as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">
        Catholic
       </a>
       , 36.2% as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical" title="Evangelical">
        evangelical
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">
        Protestant
       </a>
       , 1.3% claiming to be from other religions, including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">
        Muslims
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhists" title="Buddhists">
        Buddhists
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
        Jews
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rastafarians" title="Rastafarians">
        Rastafarians
       </a>
       , etc. and 11.1% do not belong to any religion or unresponsive. Customary Catholic church tallies and membership estimates 81% Catholic where the priest (in more than 185 parishes) is required to fill out a pastoral account of the parish each year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The CIA Factbook lists Honduras as 97% Catholic and 3% Protestant.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_1-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-cia-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Commenting on statistical variations everywhere, John Green of Pew Forum on Religion and Public Life notes that: "It isn't that ... numbers are more right than [someone else's] numbers ... but how one conceptualizes the group."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dart_100-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dart-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Often people attend one church without giving up their "home" church. Many who attend evangelical megachurches in the US, for example, attend more than one church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This shifting and fluidity is common in Brazil where two-fifths of those who were raised evangelical are no longer evangelical and Catholics seem to shift in and out of various churches, often while still remaining Catholic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most pollsters suggest an annual poll taken over a number of years would provide the best method of knowing religious demographics and variations in any single country. Still, in Honduras are thriving
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">
        Anglican
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterian" title="Presbyterian">
        Presbyterian
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">
        Methodist
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">
        Seventh-day Adventist
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">
        Lutheran
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">
        Latter-day Saint
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Mormonism" title="Mormonism">
        Mormon
       </a>
       ) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentecostal" title="Pentecostal">
        Pentecostal
       </a>
       churches. There are Protestant seminaries. The Catholic Church, still the only "church" that is recognized, is also thriving in the number of schools, hospitals, and pastoral institutions (including its own medical school) that it operates. Its
       <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">
        archbishop
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oscar_Andres_Rodriguez_Maradiaga" title="Oscar Andres Rodriguez Maradiaga">
        Oscar Andres Rodriguez Maradiaga
       </a>
       , is also very popular, both with the government, other churches, and in his own church. Practitioners of the
       <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">
        Buddhist
       </a>
       , Jewish, Islamic,
       <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">
        Bahá'í
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rastafari_movement" title="Rastafari movement">
        Rastafari
       </a>
       and indigenous denominations and religions exist.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Health">
        Health
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">
        fertility rate
       </a>
       is approximately 3.7 per woman.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The under-five mortality rate is at 40 per 1,000 live births.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_104-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The health expenditure was US$ (PPP) 197 per person in 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_104-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are about 57 physicians per 100,000 people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_104-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Honduras" title="Education in Honduras">
        Education in Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       About 83.6% of the population are
       <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">
        literate
       </a>
       and the net primary enrollment rate was 94% in 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdrstats.undp.org_104-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-hdrstats.undp.org-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2014, the primary school
       <i>
        completion
       </i>
       rate was 90.7%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Honduras has bilingual (Spanish and English) and even trilingual (Spanish with English, Arabic, and/or German)
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_schools_in_Honduras" title="List of schools in Honduras">
        schools
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Honduras" title="List of universities in Honduras">
        numerous universities
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-La_Prensa_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-La_Prensa-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The higher education is governed by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Autonomous_University_of_Honduras" title="National Autonomous University of Honduras">
        National Autonomous University of Honduras
       </a>
       which has centers in the most important cities of Honduras.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Crime">
        Crime
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Honduras" title="Crime in Honduras">
        Crime in Honduras
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Owing to insufficient law enforcement resources, crime in Honduras is rampant and criminals operate with a high degree of impunity. Consequently, Honduras has one of the highest murder rates in the world. Official statistics from the Honduran Observatory on National Violence show Honduras’ homicide rate was 60 per 100,000 in 2015 with the majority of homicide cases unprosecuted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travel_Warnings_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Travel_Warnings-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Highway assaults and carjackings at roadblocks or checkpoints set up by criminals with police uniforms and equipment occur frequently. Although reports of kidnappings of foreigners are not common, families of kidnapping victims often pay ransoms without reporting the crime to police out of fear of retribution, so kidnapping figures may be underreported.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travel_Warnings_107-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Travel_Warnings-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Owing to measures taken by government and business in 2014 to improve tourist safety, Roatan and the Bay Islands have lower crime rates than the Honduran mainland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travel_Warnings_107-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Travel_Warnings-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the less populated region of Gracias a Dios, narcotics-trafficking is rampant and police presence is scarce. Threats against U.S. citizens by drug traffickers and other criminal organizations have resulted in the U.S. Embassy placing restrictions on the travel of U.S. officials through the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travel_Warnings_107-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Travel_Warnings-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">
        Culture
       </span>
      </h2>

          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
